  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_33ae006f-9bdb-460c-93c3-da13f225560b'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

I got three things that made Prophet era is far excelent than we are now. First, They learn from Quran and Hadith directly. Second, They learn Islam to be applied in their own life, not for knowledge or discussion. The last, When they enter Islam, they left behind all jahiliah world and enter the bright Islam world. So, Let start from ourselves!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16960829685648965750"><i>Milestones</i> is a short polemical handbook for jihadist movements across the Muslim world. <br/><br/>Sayyid Qutb (1906-1966) was the leading Islamic theologian of the Egyptian Muslim Brotherhood which inspired a school of thought known as Qutbism<br/><br/>Sayyid Qutb did not like the West and even worse he did not like the East, saying that most muslim governmental systems were mired in jahiliya - a state of pre-Islamic ignorance and even barbarism.<br/><br/>He pissed off his own government so much with this book that he was i</span>
  <span id="freeText16960829685648965750" style="display:none"><i>Milestones</i> is a short polemical handbook for jihadist movements across the Muslim world. <br /><br />Sayyid Qutb (1906-1966) was the leading Islamic theologian of the Egyptian Muslim Brotherhood which inspired a school of thought known as Qutbism<br /><br />Sayyid Qutb did not like the West and even worse he did not like the East, saying that most muslim governmental systems were mired in jahiliya - a state of pre-Islamic ignorance and even barbarism.<br /><br />He pissed off his own government so much with this book that he was imprisoned and sentenced to be hanged. He rejected a clemency offer, saying his words would matter more if he was dead.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1141469686" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1141469686" />
    <a title="Charles J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9628169-charles-j"><img alt="Charles J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550495668p2/9628169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1141469686?book_show_action=true">Dec 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles J" href="/user/show/9628169-charles-j">Charles J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/20404808-milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1141469686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5468641966191456265">Milestones claims to be a revivalist primer, to return Islam to its roots, but it is really a hybrid of traditional Islam with modern ideological organizing systems, primarily Leninism. This hybrid has proven to be a powerful combination, perhaps unsurprisingly given the power of both Leninism and modern Islamism in the 20th Century. Qutb’s contribution was to meld the two, promising the fusion will create harmonious societies with unparalleled virtues equivalent to those of the first Islamic so</span>
  <span id="freeText5468641966191456265" style="display:none">Milestones claims to be a revivalist primer, to return Islam to its roots, but it is really a hybrid of traditional Islam with modern ideological organizing systems, primarily Leninism. This hybrid has proven to be a powerful combination, perhaps unsurprisingly given the power of both Leninism and modern Islamism in the 20th Century. Qutb’s contribution was to meld the two, promising the fusion will create harmonious societies with unparalleled virtues equivalent to those of the first Islamic societies. This is the core of modern, late 20th-century Islamism, of which Qutb was its primary theoretician.<br /><br />Revivalism in Islam has a long history. The original revivalists were the Kharijites of the 7th century AD, who like Qutb believed that other Muslims had lost their way and were no longer Muslims, and were therefore apostates worthy of death. Kharijites believed, among other things, that any Muslim who sinned and failed to repent was no longer a Muslim (similar to certain the early Christian heresy Montanism, and to the Christian heresy of Donatism as applied to sacramental efficacy of priests).<br /><br />In Marshall Hodgson’s encyclopedic The Venture Of Islam, the Kharijites are described in detail, and it’s instructive to view Qutb through that historical prism. As they say, everything old is new again—the Kharijites were primarily young, unattached men looking for transcendence and meaning in their life, which they found in a combination of an ascetic, purified version of their religion combined with the violence attractive to young men throughout history. Unsurprisingly, as with modern Islamists, the Kharijites were unpopular with both established governments, who did not appreciate being called illegitimate, and the majority of Muslims, who did not appreciate being characterized as apostates, but as with most hardcore ideological movements, a relatively small group was able to make a substantial impact before their influence died away. But, of course, variations crop up reliably, most recently with 19th-century Wahhabism, still the dominant force in Saudi Arabia today, due to its fusion with the state.<br /><br />Leninism in Islam, on the other hand, has no history at all before Qutb. Milestones emphasizes the critical nature of the vanguard of Islamic believers in converting an unbelieving society. Qutb shows how the original Muslims were a vanguard in their pagan society, and rather than converting those around them with arguments from reason, or arguments about how Islam led to a better life, they instead showed by the example of their lives and their complete submission to Allah that the only way possible for human happiness is Islam. Also as with Leninism, Qutb endorses that the vanguard may use violence to achieve the objective of creating a true Muslim society—“propaganda of the deed,” in Leninist terms. It’s a commonplace that Communism is a religion, but it shows most clearly in these sorts of parallels.<br /><br />Qutb rejects reforming or organizing a society around any other principle than Islam. Imperialism, economic justice, racial justice, and so forth are not relevant considerations with regard to reforming a society. Qutb would say that a Muslim society is ideal on all social measures, but the goal is not to achieve any social measure, only to be a pure Muslim society, which by definition will be perfect in all ways possible on Earth, and lead to happiness for believers in the hereafter.<br /><br />Of course, as with most revival movements, but with the extra helping of dualism inherent in Islam, this is a very Manichean view. Oddly, Milestones has something in common with Roman Catholic publications like the Baltimore Catechism—it is a self-contained vision of the world, which offers its own justification without the need for internally coherent reasoning. Islam is true because it is true. It is the best because it is the best.<br /><br />Qutb categorizes all modern (as of the 1960s) Muslim societies as non-Muslim, or jahiliyyah (a term originally applied to the pagans of the Arabian peninsula prior to the rise to power of Muhammad, and to the Mongols, who accepted Islam but didn’t bother to actually follow any of the rules). He also categorizes all non-Muslim societies in the same way. To him, both are just as in need of being converted to true Muslim societies, through the leadership of the vanguard.<br /><br />Other than the perfection of Islam and its need for human happiness, what comes through most clearly in Milestones is an emphasis on the triumphalism, necessary superiority and required dominance of Islam. This is a mainstream Muslim belief, not Qutb’s or Islamists’ alone. For Qutb, and for most mainstream Muslims, it is not necessary that everybody be Muslim, for religion is each person’s choice (though it is absolutely necessary to be Muslim for that person to achieve Heaven). As the Qur’an is quoted, “There is no compulsion in religion.” However, it is absolutely necessary in each and every society that Muslims be in charge and that Islam be completely dominant. This dominance is defined as the equivalent of total freedom for everyone. No society is permissible that is not wholly within the daily total control of Muslims and only Muslims, for only then are all men free. “[Non-Muslim] societies do not give [Islam] any opportunity to organize its followers according to its own method, and hence it is the duty of Islam to annihilate all such systems, as they are obstacles in the way of universal freedom.” And, “wherever an Islamic community exists, it has a God-given right to step forward and take control of the political authority so it may establish the Divine system on earth, while it leaves the matter of belief to individual conscience.” This is very different, of course, from the Western idea of freedom of conscience combined with democracy.<br /><br />Qutb also believes that individual Muslims should focus on their personal, individual superiority to non-Muslims. Chapter 11, “The Faith Triumphant,” has such gems as “Conditions change, the Muslim loses his physical power and is conquered, yet the consciousness does not depart from him that he is the most superior.” This is an interesting comparison to Christianity, which theologically emphasizes the need to NOT feel superior to any other human beings, the sin of pride, despite the deep human tendency toward pride. From Qutb’s vantage, though, Muslim societies had descended from their prior ascendency, and he is keen to emphasize that Islam does not need to be justified or favored because of worldly success, scientific inventions, or any other worldly evidence. It is superior because it is superior.<br /><br />Milestones is a powerful book. Yes, there are howlers (The Jews “penetrate into the body politic of the whole world and then [are] free to perpetrate their evil designs”! Islam created the scientific method and Europeans stole it!) But its vision is compelling in ways that may not be fully appreciated by the closed-minded reader. Islam offers a vision of social harmony, where all people are equal—not just in the sense of no racial or economic distinctions, but where the king is actually equal to the peasant. Sure, in practice that may not be fully true, or true at all, but Muslim societies come closest to this ideal of any societies in history. Those who think of Islam as merely a violent religion offering nothing fail to appreciate the beauty of much of what Islam has created, the refined thinking of thousands of keen theologians over 1500 years, and the compelling, simple to understand theological vision Islam offers (no need to bother with paradoxes like the Christian Trinity!) And a dualistic, good/evil approach that offers a total solution to life here and life hereafter has a compelling pull to many people who seek transcendence. It’s no wonder that Qutb’s combination of this powerful vision with modern ideological organizing principles has proven so powerful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5468641966191456265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_193229626" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/193229626" />
    <a title="Duane Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5940159-duane-miller"><img alt="Duane Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547205910p2/5940159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193229626?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane Miller" href="/user/show/5940159-duane-miller">Duane Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193229626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15259849588360385983">Qutb (1906-1966) was born in Egypt and was, and still is, a guiding light for the Muslim Brotherhood. Since the Muslim Brotherhood now controls Egypt and Gaza, it is a good time to become more familiar with his ideas. In this book he formulates his position that all the world, including so-called Muslim states, are in a state of <i>jahiliyya</i> or ignorance. He proposes a way to resolve this problem and see the entire world, eventually, submitting to the benevolent will of its Creator in the form of t</span>
  <span id="freeText15259849588360385983" style="display:none">Qutb (1906-1966) was born in Egypt and was, and still is, a guiding light for the Muslim Brotherhood. Since the Muslim Brotherhood now controls Egypt and Gaza, it is a good time to become more familiar with his ideas. In this book he formulates his position that all the world, including so-called Muslim states, are in a state of <i>jahiliyya</i> or ignorance. He proposes a way to resolve this problem and see the entire world, eventually, submitting to the benevolent will of its Creator in the form of the shari'a.<br /><br />I can see why this book ended up being so influential for Muslims around the world. It is certainly a call to arms and a return to Qutb's erroneous and fantastic version of early Islam. His main problem is history--he has little understanding of what actually occurred in the days of the so-called Rightly-guided Caliphs (al khulafa' al rashiduun). His mythical 'golden age' is not so different than how fundamentalist Christians view the Apostolic Church. The difference between those two origin myths though can be summarized in one phrase: the sword of the religion (sayf id diin).<br /><br />The originality of Milestones is Qutb's incorporation of the (imported) revolutionary concept of the vanguard, so important to, for instance, the Bolsheviks. He proposed the formation of a vanguard which by its example and piety and voluntary submission to the will of Allah would attract people to itself, eventually resulting in the rebirth of a true Islamic civilization in dar al islam, which would eventually spread throughout the entire world. The Muslim Brotherhood was the first such vanguard. And today it controls Egypt and Gaza and has outposts throughout the world, including in the form of CAIR (Council on American-Islamic Relations) in the USA.<br /><br />This short book is well worth your time. Not because it is particularly well-written, but because it has been extremely influential and continues to shape our world today. If Qutb is right, the time of jahiliyya for the most populous nation in the Middle East is now over, and the true reign of God has come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15259849588360385983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1303135903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1303135903" />
    <a title="Maica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41289357-maica"><img alt="Maica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563541204p2/41289357.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_1739373680" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1739373680" />
    <a title="Tengku Ahmad Hazri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17057617-tengku-ahmad-hazri"><img alt="Tengku Ahmad Hazri" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1739373680?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tengku Ahmad Hazri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tengku Ahmad Hazri" href="/user/show/17057617-tengku-ahmad-hazri">Tengku Ahmad Hazri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17057617-tengku-ahmad-hazri?shelf=islam">islam</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1739373680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17986985017948557759">Sayyid Qutb was foreign to the public consciousness before the events of September 11 and his Milestones hardly ever made it to the reading list of students of political theory. Whereas for many Muslims his perseverance in the face of Nasser's dictatorship was a source of inspiration, scholars in the west have found it fit to include him as the ideologue of Islamic terrorism. Yet after September 11, conspiracy theories unleashed by the mainstream media barely a week after the tragedy explicitly </span>
  <span id="freeText17986985017948557759" style="display:none">Sayyid Qutb was foreign to the public consciousness before the events of September 11 and his Milestones hardly ever made it to the reading list of students of political theory. Whereas for many Muslims his perseverance in the face of Nasser's dictatorship was a source of inspiration, scholars in the west have found it fit to include him as the ideologue of Islamic terrorism. Yet after September 11, conspiracy theories unleashed by the mainstream media barely a week after the tragedy explicitly traced al-Qaeda's ideology back to him and a vast series of nomenclature had surfaced ever since describing him from a "philosopher of Islamic terror" (New York Times Magazine) to an advocate of "anarcho-Islam" (The Guardian).<br /><br />Milestones, however, is far from the "Islamic anarchy" manifesto it is made out to be, although a superficial reading would have easily deceived the gullible reader to this effect. The essence of this work is neatly summarised in the first sentence of the book, that "mankind today is on the brink of a precipice" owing to the loss of spirituality in modern life and this descent into self-destruction is inescapable unless man returns to his spiritual foundations found in Islam. This force that robs mankind off its spiritual power he calls Jahiliyyah (ignorance, often attributed to pre-Islamic Arabia), fashioned after Maududi, but redefined so that the notion of "partial Jahiliyyah" is now gone. The world according to him is now in an epic confrontation between Islam and Jahiliyyah but even within the Islamic Ummah, Muslims are gradually sinking deeper into Jahiliyyah. Qutb was thus sceptical that the true teaching of Islam could be revived by the entire community of believers, so he proposed for a formation of a vanguard to defend the true face of Islam: "Islamic belief should at once materialise into a practical movement, and from the very instant this comes into being, the movement should become a real representation and an accurate mirror of its belief" (Chapter 2).<br /><br />This vanguard must never be confused with the Leninist model of political subversion, for this implies somehow that it must first seize state power and then impose Islamisation from above. On the contrary, Qutb is at pain to point out that "a Muslim community can come into existence only when individuals and groups of people reject servitude to anyone except God" (Chapter 5). Change, in other words, must come from the people, who would then form a community, and then a state, which would then provide the necessary leadership to bring the entire human race out of Jahiliyyah. Above all else, faith must first be imprinted into the hearts and minds of the Muslims who would become this vanguard: "The stage of constructing the faith should be long, and it should be gradual" (Chapter 2).<br /><br />For Qutb, Jahiliyyah is characterised by man (not God) making laws for man, so it constitutes "man's lordship over man" and it is the goal of Islam to free mankind from this mortal slavery. Central to this work is the message behind the proclamation "There is no god but God" and Qutb dedicated an entire chapter on this point alone. He was inspired by the story - told by Ibn Kathir - of Muslim soldiers who entered the camp of the Persian general Rustum and boldy declared their objective to free people from "man's lordship over man." Consistent with his notion that "Islam is a universal declaration of the freedom of man on earth from every authority except God's authority" (Chapter 4) and the universality of its message, Jihad (struggle) cannot be a mere tool for self-defence but a constant effort from preaching and argument to actual fighting: "If we insist on calling Islamic Jihad a defensive movement, then we must change the meaning of the word 'defence' and mean by it 'the defence of man' against all those elements which limit his freedom" (Chapter 4).<br /><br />Contrary to popular myth, he never exalted physical aggression as the dominant feature of jihad. His approach is pragmatic, as he believed Islam is, so that when force is used to prevent the peaceful propagation of Islam, it is right that force is equally used to remove this impediment. And since he deemed the entire Muslim world as one community, this method doesn't stop at the national borders, so that when physical force is used against Muslims in one country, other Muslims states are duty-bound to interfere and protect them. In short, where freedom of propagating Islam is secured, the military jihad ought not to be resorted to.<br /><br />Probably the greatest setback of this book is Qutb's extremist epistemology, his inability to see beyond polar opposites, beyond black-and-white. Thus for him, "in the world there is only one party of God; all else are parties of Satan and rebellion" (Chapter 9). Even his juxtaposition of Dar-al-Islam (which he restrictively defined) against Dar-al-Harb is at odds with traditional Islam - Abu Hanifah had a rather flexible conception of Dar-al-Islam while as-Shafie recognised a third category of Dar-al-Sulh.<br /><br />Qutb's aim is to revive what is regarded by many as the "Golden Age" of Islam, namely the first generation of pious Muslims, from Muhammad to the last Caliph Ali. To this end, he persisted that the method used by the Prophet is exactly followed. He dreams of a utopia, if not nostalgic world, one where "nationalism here is belief, homeland here is Dar-al-Islam, the ruler here is God, and the constitution here is the Quran" (Chapter 9).</span>
  <a data-text-id="17986985017948557759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1360512939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360512939" />
    <a title="Azwa Ahmad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20581922-azwa-ahmad"><img alt="Azwa Ahmad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577945622p2/20581922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360512939?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Azwa Ahmad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Azwa Ahmad" href="/user/show/20581922-azwa-ahmad">Azwa Ahmad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/15749400-milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360512939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer948251122998085005">1. This is a call for a total departure from Jahiliyyah system and its influences in order to establish an Islamic order in its entirety.<br/><br/>2. To depart, one must first understand the real meaning of the first part of the shahadah of ‘There’s no deity worthy of worship but Allah’.<br/><br/>3. The real meaning being: a total submission to Allah. Be it in terms of our faith and belief, ways of life, values and standards, morals and manners, or the laws and regulations ordained by Allah.<br/><br/>4. It took 13 painstakin</span>
  <span id="freeText948251122998085005" style="display:none">1. This is a call for a total departure from Jahiliyyah system and its influences in order to establish an Islamic order in its entirety.<br /><br />2. To depart, one must first understand the real meaning of the first part of the shahadah of ‘There’s no deity worthy of worship but Allah’.<br /><br />3. The real meaning being: a total submission to Allah. Be it in terms of our faith and belief, ways of life, values and standards, morals and manners, or the laws and regulations ordained by Allah.<br /><br />4. It took 13 painstakingly long years in Meccan period to establish this belief of ‘There’s no deity worthy of worship but Allah’.<br /><br />5. Hence, one must understand that only through the real understanding of this belief, a Muslim society and a Muslim community that is ruled by the Shariah laws could be established.<br /><br />6. Only with the real understanding can one submit in totality to Allah.<br /><br />7. Real understanding of the first part of the shahadah would bring about a total submission to Allah, which means we believe that the authority of this world should be given back to Allah, the Creator; to acknowledge and admit His sovereignty over the world; to realise that only He is worthy and have the right to rule the world, not His creations.<br /><br />8. To admit His sovereignty would lead us to accept all of His injunctions and orders, including His decreed laws: the Shariah laws.<br /><br />9. This is what it means by submitting to Allah in full, to understand the real meaning of ‘There’s no deity worthy of worship but Allah’.<br /><br />10. After the 13 years of hard work to solidify the first part of the shahadah, the Muslims migrated to Medina and created the first Muslim society. This was when Allah then revealed the Medinite verses, with its laws and orders, in order to serve the now established Muslim society.<br /><br />“When a group of people enter this religion in the true sense, only then can it be considered a ‘Muslim group’. Only such a group has the capability of giving a concrete form to the Islamic system in its social life, because such a group has agreed to base its entire life on Islam and to obey Allah Almighty in all aspects of life. Thus, when such a society gradually comes into being and the basic teachings of Islam are its guide, it will proceed to formulate laws and regulations for the existing needs according to the general teachings of Islam. This is the correct order for bringing about a practical, realistic and wise Islamic system." pg.64<br /><br />11. Islam comes to free human beings from being enslaved to other human beings, it comes with one objective: that is to set them free and return the sovereignty back to Allah from the hands of the human lords.<br /><br />12. Islam is a universal declaration of the freedom of man of the earth from every authority except that of Allah, freeing people from their servitude to other men and bring them into the service of Allah.<br /><br />13. The subjugation or enslavement of one human being to another would only subject one to another’s tyranny, oppression and exploitation –as what we now see.<br /><br />14. The confrontation, refutation, or the challenge against Islam is ultimately a war against this belief: of a belief that the sovereignty of this world should rest upon Allah Almighty alone and none of the creations have the right to claim this position.<br /><br />15. Once such a claim is established and propounded, the tyrants or the usurpers of Allah’s sovereignty would be the first to challenge this claim –as what had happened during the Meccan period when the call towards Islam was first made.<br /><br />16. It is a natural response, given that Islam comes to take away their authority in this world and hand it over to Allah Almighty.<br /><br />17. Taking away their authority means taking away all privileges that comes with it: in terms of their ideologies, their own standards and values, their system of life, their laws and regulations -all mad-made forms that are imposed by these human lords on their subjects.<br /><br />18. Islam comes to take away all these and re-establish our way of life and our system of life according to Allah’s orders and injunctions.<br /><br />19. In the Quran, our faith and belief, standards and values, morals and manner, the way of life, the system of life, the laws and regulations have been laid out for us to follow.<br /><br />20. Rather than following the man-made creations, we follow what is divinely revealed to us in the Quran.<br /><br />21. A total submission to Allah would bring about the acknowledgment of His authority not just in this world, but of heaven and earth, thus we follow what He has revealed to us in the Quran and through Muhammad saw (the second part of the shahadah).<br /><br />22. We, the Muslims, must follow every single part that has been revealed to us in the Quran, including the implementation of the Shariah laws in this world. (Note: Sayyid Qutb seemed to be stressing on this particular matter on a great degree)<br /><br />23. Only by accepting all these that we can claim we acknowledge the authority and sovereignty of Allah Almighty, the All-Wise, All-Knowing, in this world.<br /><br />24. Now, to challenge the Jahiliyyah system and its influences in our lives, Islam can’t be constricted to be as an individual struggle only.<br /><br />25. The Jahiliyyah movement must be confronted and challenged by an active, dynamic and progressive distinct movement that is of equal or superior in strength and quality than that of the Jahiliyyah movement – a better alternative so to say.<br /><br />26. Islamic movement then is a social responsibility or a collective effort of a Muslim community to challenge this Jahiliyyah movement, its system and influences, replacing with something better – Islamic orders; with its faith and belief, standards and values, moral and manners, its way of life and system of life, with its laws and regulations that is the Shariah laws.<br /><br />27. Islam, however, will only be a better alternative, a better way of life and a better system of life, ONLY when the adherents, the followers, the believers, the Muslims become the perfect embodiment of its teachings and exemplifying the Quranic teachings to the greatest degree with their utmost effort.<br /><br />28. If not, the so-called Muslim society or Islamic nations we now see, is nothing but part of the Jahiliyyah society and nations. These so-called Muslim society and nations do not represent the true sense of the Islamic and Quranic teachings.<br /><br />29. This is why no Islamic movement can challenge the perverse Jahiliyyah movement, given the inferior quality of the ‘Muslims’ community can never give birth to an Islamic movement that is of a better quality than the Jahiliyyah’s.<br /><br />30. This is why no Muslim individuals or community manage to achieve the same standard as the first Muslim individuals and community during the 7th century.<br /><br />31. Because our source of belief has been mixed with those that are man-made, and we do not source it purely from the Quranic teachings unlike the first Muslims.<br /><br />"In later times it happened that other sources mingled with it. Other sources used by later generations included Greek philosophy and logic, ancient Persian legends and their ideas, Jewish scriptures and traditions, Christian theology, and in addition to these, fragments of other religions and civilizations. These mingled with the commentaries on the Holy Quran and with scholastic theology, as they were mingled with jurisprudence and its principles." pg.41<br /><br />32. The first Muslims are the perfect embodiment and the right representatives of the Quranic teachings, which later constructed a Muslim community of an unparalleled might and quality.<br /><br />33. Their Lord is only Allah and the source of belief and training was the Quran.<br /><br />34. They believed that Islam is the true faith, the true religion.<br /><br />35. They had the greatest confidence in Islam, while we do not.<br /><br />36. They did not compromise with the Jahiliyyah system; they knew that the truth could never co-exist or mixed with the falsehood. While we compromise and allow them to co-exist.<br /><br />37. They saw Jahiliyyah system and its influences void of values and morals; theirs standards changed according to their desires; and their lifestyles degenerated themselves to animalistic behavior. While we, on the other hand, worship such influences and the kind of lifestyle promoted by the Jahiliyyah.<br /><br />38. How can we ever change the Jahiliyyah system and how can we ever remove its influences in our lives if we fail to see the perfection of Islam and hold the greatest confidence in its all-encompassing teachings, revealed by Allah Almighty, our only Lord?<br /><br />39. Now that I have mentioned all these, I must not forget that Sayyid Qutb, time and again, reminded the readers that there is no compulsion in religion (Surah Al-Baqarah, 2:256).<br /><br />40. Islam is a universal declaration of freedom, to free human beings from servitude to other men.<br /><br />41. This call for freedom –the same as we now see- is important so that people can practice freedom of religion.<br /><br />42. With this freedom of religion, the call to Islam and the spread of Islamic teachings could be done freely with no interventions.<br /><br />“There shall be no compulsion in (acceptance of) the religion. The right course has become clear from the wrong. So whoever disbelieves in Taghut and believes in Allah has grasped the most trustworthy handhold with no break in it. And Allah is Hearing and Knowing.” (2:256)<br /><br />43. People will have a choice either to accept or reject Islam.<br /><br />44. Islam is the truth, and people will accept it when they recognize it.<br /><br />45. So we, the so-called Muslim individuals or the so-called Muslim society, must embody and be the representative of the Quranic teachings in the best manner, so that the non-Muslims can see the beauty of this faith and the perfection of its teachings and system of life that Allah has created for us.<br /><br />46. And like what Ustadh Nouman Ali Khan said, we must remember that Islam comes as a mercy to the humankind. It doesn’t come to punish people. It is here to save us from all kind of destruction we now see.<br /><br />:)</span>
  <a data-text-id="948251122998085005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3637023552" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3637023552" />
    <a title="Bryan Metzger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/116339091-bryan-metzger"><img alt="Bryan Metzger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594506085p2/116339091.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3637023552?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan Metzger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan Metzger" href="/user/show/116339091-bryan-metzger">Bryan Metzger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3637023552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3109930666948767395">This was kind of an impulse buy I made in the Spring of 2019, when I was doing a tutorial at Oxford on the Gulf states. I'd become vaguely interested in the roots of Islamism and wanted to see what I could glean from this text, given its stature as one of the foundational texts. Now that I've finally read it, it turns out there's not a whole lot to be gleaned.<br/><br/>Rather than providing a pathway towards building an Islamic society, Qutb spends much of his time waging a polemic, creating delineations </span>
  <span id="freeText3109930666948767395" style="display:none">This was kind of an impulse buy I made in the Spring of 2019, when I was doing a tutorial at Oxford on the Gulf states. I'd become vaguely interested in the roots of Islamism and wanted to see what I could glean from this text, given its stature as one of the foundational texts. Now that I've finally read it, it turns out there's not a whole lot to be gleaned.<br /><br />Rather than providing a pathway towards building an Islamic society, Qutb spends much of his time waging a polemic, creating delineations between that which he considers Islamic and that which he considers to be Jahiliyyah, or "ignorance." In Qutb's view, no society on Earth at the time of his writing fit the bill, and all extant societies-- capitalist, communist, even those that already purported to be Islamic-- were all mired to varying degrees in Jahiliyyah. This is because they allow for the "rule of men over other men," and only through a return to a close interpretation of the Quran and the sovereignty of Allah-- as was the case in the early days of Islam in the Arabian peninsula-- can this be changed. Qutb focuses almost exclusively on the need to live according to the teachings of the Quran and of Shari'ah, arguing that the specific organizational structure of the resulting society will sort itself out. In my view, it seems that Qutb is laying out a vision which no society can reasonably achieve, leaving even those societies which purport to be Islamic open to religious legitimacy challenges. Indeed, this is the subtext of Saudi Arabia's present aversion to the Muslim Brotherhood.<br /><br />Qutb does say some interesting things about science, including that learning things like chemistry, astronomy, physics and so on from Western sources is not un-Islamic, so long as it does not veer into "speculative philosophy." In other words, science cannot be allowed to challenge what the Quran might say about the origins of human existence, or of the universe.<br /><br />The text is repetitive, which makes it easy to breeze through the book in a few hours but also leaves the reader somewhat frustrated. Read if you're interested in the topic, but otherwise you're not bound to learn all that much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3109930666948767395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_135413165" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/135413165" />
    <a title="Halik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4146153-halik"><img alt="Halik" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/135413165?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Halik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Halik" href="/user/show/4146153-halik">Halik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9695487">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146153-halik?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146153-halik?shelf=geopolitics">geopolitics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146153-halik?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135413165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8751638825246973931">The only treatise of 'political Islam' (using 'political' here is really redundant, as this book will tell you) i have read, other than the Qur'an of course. The Muslim Brotherhood, a movement of which Qutb is considered to be one of the main intellectuals, is the main opposition party in man countries in the middle east and North Africa. It originated in the early 20th century and was started by a schoolteacher initially as a community center for Egyptian boys to get them away from the corrupti</span>
  <span id="freeText8751638825246973931" style="display:none">The only treatise of 'political Islam' (using 'political' here is really redundant, as this book will tell you) i have read, other than the Qur'an of course. The Muslim Brotherhood, a movement of which Qutb is considered to be one of the main intellectuals, is the main opposition party in man countries in the middle east and North Africa. It originated in the early 20th century and was started by a schoolteacher initially as a community center for Egyptian boys to get them away from the corrupting influence of colonial Egyptian society.<br /><br />The movement quickly gathered force and metamorphosized into a political organization that campaigned for pan-Islamism as opposed to secularism and nation-statism; two key ideologies that were beginning to emerge in the Islamic world then, along with Wahabbism.<br /><br />The Islamists envision a peaceful revival of the religion of Islam in a community of Muslims prepared to 'reject the authority of man and submit completely to the authority of God'. Qutb mainly talks about his vision for social reform and gives extensive Quranic evidence and evidence from the life and practices of Prophet Muhammed, peace be upon him, to back himself up. He espouses that most of the world, including modern secular and supposedly non secular Islamic state, are seeped in a sense of jahiliyyah (ignorance) and are in a spiraling moral crisis. He emphasizes that Islam is the only philosophy, law and moral code that can fix this and is the only system that can provide true well being to human beings.<br /><br />Many say that Qutb and his teachings were a key inspiration to todays 'Islamic terrorist' groups such as Al-Quaeda etc. But Milestones calls only for a peaceful propagation of the religion. The first thing it calls for is internal change, changing Muslim society itself. Making it more adherent to the laws of God and calling it to submit to Him and only Him. He then categorizes the outside world into two categories. Namely the non-Muslim nations at peace with Muslims and the Non-Muslim nations at war with it. <br /><br />This may seem a little out there and intense until you realize that Qutb's definition of the nation of Muslims encompasses a nationalism of its own, as in a member of that nation will not feel any loyalty to any geographical country but will only be loyal to the nation of Islam. So in that context it becomes like any other country in the world today, those with enemies and those with not. But the key to remember, says Qutb, is that the nation of Islam is fundamentally subject to God's laws and its citizens are free to choose and believe as they will. He insists that modern capitalists and socialist state structures have largely deprived their citizen's ability to choose. But for an Islamic state such as this to be created, he says, enough people must first collectively agree to be part of a state such as that.<br /><br />I would recommend it to all Muslims and no-Muslims alike. It provides a fascinating view of how Islam can work in the socio-political and economic environment of today's world and certainly reveals its timeless applicability.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8751638825246973931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3256553085" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3256553085" />
    <a title="John Aggrey Odera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44535036-john-aggrey-odera"><img alt="John Aggrey Odera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581949049p2/44535036.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3256553085?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Aggrey Odera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Aggrey Odera" href="/user/show/44535036-john-aggrey-odera">John Aggrey Odera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44535036-john-aggrey-odera?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44535036-john-aggrey-odera?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44535036-john-aggrey-odera?shelf=islam-and-muslims">islam-and-muslims</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3256553085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15957430189002180691">I became interested in Qutb after hearing him invoked severally in discussions as the ideologue behind the doctrines animating the Islamic State. Qutb argues here that “mankind is on the brink of a precipice” because humanity is “devoid of those vital values which are necessary not only for its healthy development, but also for its real progress”. While he recognizes that Western civilization has brought forth great technological advancement and material prosperity, Qutb contends that in the rea</span>
  <span id="freeText15957430189002180691" style="display:none">I became interested in Qutb after hearing him invoked severally in discussions as the ideologue behind the doctrines animating the Islamic State. Qutb argues here that “mankind is on the brink of a precipice” because humanity is “devoid of those vital values which are necessary not only for its healthy development, but also for its real progress”. While he recognizes that Western civilization has brought forth great technological advancement and material prosperity, Qutb contends that in the realm of values, what he takes to be the significant force behind mankind, western civilization has failed. <br /><br />The problem, Qutb argues, is that mankind, led by the western man, has commited Jahilliyah (rebellion against divine guidance), and has declared himself sovereign over all the earth. The result of this is that men have become rulers over one another; the rulers prescribe rules and determine behaviours and systems not according to God’s will, but as they see it fit. As a result, God’s creatures are oppressed in this system. “Thus the humiliation of man under the communist system, and the exploitation of nations and individuals due to greed for wealth and imperialism under the capitalist systems are but a corollary of rebellion against God’s authority and the denial of the dignity of man given to him by God”.<br /><br />Qutb’s solution to this problem of Jahilliyah is that Islam must gain leadership over all of mankind, and redirect it towards recognizing God as sovereign. He insists that Islam must offer more than material progress in this endeavor (though it must also be careful not to shun it; material progress is good). Islam must offer “a way of life which on the one hand conserves the benefits of science and technology, and on the other hand fulfills human needs on the same level of excellence as technology has fulfilled them on the sphere of material comfort”. Islam is uniquely placed to assume this leadership, Qutb insists, for only in Islam are all men free from the servitude of other men, and dedicated to that of God alone.<br /><br />But this Islamic way of life must manifest itself in a real nation/ society with real people - thus there has to be a muslim society. For this to happen, there necessarily must be a vanguard; a class of enlightened muslims who bring forth this Islamic society. The vanguard should “know the landmarks and the milestones of the road towards this goal so that they may recognize the starting place, the nature, the responsibility, and the ultimate purpose of this long journey”. These milestones must of course be determined from the primary source of Islam - The Holy Qur’an.<br /><br />It makes sense how from these arguments, ideologues like Baghdadi arose, seeing themselves as the vanguard destined to bring Islamic leadership to the world, and the Islamic State as the logical culmination of Qutb’s ideas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15957430189002180691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2022355902" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2022355902" />
    <a title="Luis C" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17939495-luis-c"><img alt="Luis C" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362828209p2/17939495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2022355902?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luis C" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luis C" href="/user/show/17939495-luis-c">Luis C</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2022355902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17886689896672914166">Not exactly what I use to read, this was nonetheless fascinating. Be aware that I'm not an expert in Islam, so I'm supposing that Qutb's narrative is his opinion, but it could be that it's a mainstream islamic interpretation. Be warned.<br/><br/>Starting with the style, it's atrocious. The author goes on and on over the same concepts and gets really tiresome. This book could be 30 pages long at best, but is far longer in reality. In fact, if you exclude the last two chapters, and maybe the first one, the </span>
  <span id="freeText17886689896672914166" style="display:none">Not exactly what I use to read, this was nonetheless fascinating. Be aware that I'm not an expert in Islam, so I'm supposing that Qutb's narrative is his opinion, but it could be that it's a mainstream islamic interpretation. Be warned.<br /><br />Starting with the style, it's atrocious. The author goes on and on over the same concepts and gets really tiresome. This book could be 30 pages long at best, but is far longer in reality. In fact, if you exclude the last two chapters, and maybe the first one, the rest are basically a rehash of the same idea.<br /><br />What's this idea? Qutb's main argument is that Islam is summed up in the Shahada: "There's no God but Allah, and Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah". From here, Qutb affirms, everything in Islam is derived. He interprets the phrase that "there's no God but Allah" as saying that only Allah can and should have power over man. So, for Qutb, that means that the radicality of Islam is to make man totally free, even from Islam. So a free society is the one where people are free, meaning that no man have power over him, and no law power over him except the Sharia.<br /><br />So there's a strange interplay of freedom and compulsion, because, as we know, religious systems (as Islam is) are usually very normative. So don't think Qutb is rooting for libertatian anarchism: he's just wishing that the believers in Islam were so pure as to autoorganise based on the Quran's guidance. (That's not exactly true, Qutb admits in parts of his book that there are people who have to teach others to interpret the Quran correctly).<br /><br />Obviusly, and this is perhaps Qutb's second point, there are no societies in the world right now that conforms to this standard, so Qutb concludes that there are no true believers right now, and all of the world's population are in a state of Jahiliyyah (ignorance, previous to Muhammad's revelation, here used by Qutb as an oposition of the practice of the Quran).<br /><br />The black or white view permeates the book and is what makes it so attractive. It's narrative is very simple and manichean: you're either with Allah, or not; you're either a Muslim or not; you're either a believer and thus good, wise, etc.; or an unbeliever and therefore wicked and evil.<br /><br />There's a very interesting incorporation of the Leninist vanguard concept. In Qutb's opinion, the way to implement Islam in the world is not through preaching, but through living. So this vanguard, having understood the concepts of Islam, and living in this free and true Islamic society, would live such perfects lives that people will come to learn and to start living like them.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17886689896672914166" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_41782834" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41782834" />
    <a title="Andreas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/667938-andreas"><img alt="Andreas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1197555863p2/667938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41782834?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andreas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andreas" href="/user/show/667938-andreas">Andreas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41782834" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11293889880624523230">"The fundamental malaise of modern Islam is a sense that something has gone wrong with its history". Such were the words written by Wilfred Cantwell Smith fifty years ago, and there is something in them that should speak to us even today. <br/><br/>Sayyid Qutb (or however his name should be transcribed, it is spelled in three different ways in the course of the first three pages of the book) is said to be perhaps the most influential Arab philosopher of the 20th Century. His influence is certainly not dim</span>
  <span id="freeText11293889880624523230" style="display:none">"The fundamental malaise of modern Islam is a sense that something has gone wrong with its history". Such were the words written by Wilfred Cantwell Smith fifty years ago, and there is something in them that should speak to us even today. <br /><br />Sayyid Qutb (or however his name should be transcribed, it is spelled in three different ways in the course of the first three pages of the book) is said to be perhaps the most influential Arab philosopher of the 20th Century. His influence is certainly not diminished by the immense impact he had on the thinking of a young Egyptian doctor back in the 1980s. Ayman al-Zawahiri radicalised Qutb's thoughts about the need for an Islamic vanguard and built the philosophy of what is today known as al-Quaida on him. That alone should be reason enough to make this book relevant even today. <br /><br />In short, Qutb makes three points in this book. First, the whole modern world is in a state of Jahiliyya. This ancient Arabic term is sometimes used to describe the period before Muhammad had his revelations and brought the light of civilization to the world, whereas in Qutb's writing it is used to describe a state where mankind has strayed from the ways of God even against better knowledge. Qutb describes the ensuing 'first generation' of Muslims in glorious terms and calls for the whole world to return to this state of God's rule on earth. <br /><br />Second, he describes the means through which this should happen. Islam's goal, he claims, is "to abolish those oppressive political systems under which people are prevented from expressing their freedom to choose whatever beliefs they want, and after that it gives them complete freedom to decide wether they will acccept Islam or not". Showing the mentality with which every true Muslim would approach this task, he quotes three Arab generals during a campaign against the Persians. When asked by the Persian general the purpose of their arrival, the Arab generals gave the same answer: <br /><br />"God has sent us to bring anyone who wishes from servitude to men into the service of God alone, from the narrowness of this world into the vastness of this world and the Hereafter, and from the tyranny of religions into the justice of Islam. God raised a Messenger for this purpose to teach His creatures His way. If anyone accepts this way of life, we turn back and give his country back to him, and we fight with those who rebel until we are martyred or become victorious".<br /><br />The justification for this, Qutb finds in the Quran:<br />"Fight against those among the People of the book who do not believe in God and the Last Day, who do not forbid what God and His Messenger have forbidden, and who do not consider the true religion as their religion, until they are subdued and pay Jizyah"<br /><br />Now, at this point the reader may be inclined to believe that Islam is a violent religion, bent on forcing its beliefs upon people via the sword. This, however, could not be more wrong. Qutb states that "the orientalists have painted a picture of Islam as a violent movement which imposed a belief upon people by the sword. The vicious orientalists know very well that this is not true, but by this method they try to distort the true motives of Islamic Jihaad". What Islam wants is merely to destroy those oppressive political systems which stand between men and God and hinder them from making a completely free choice of whether to accept Islam or not. Implicit in this claim is of course the notion that once given the possibility to make a completely free choice, most humans will embrace Islam with no need to invoke force. Qutb cites historical examples to bring home his point:<br /><br />"Considering [the people with which there were peace treaties:], it was commanded that they be given four months' notice of expiration, at the end of which they should be considered open enemies and fought with. Thus, those who broke the treaty were fought against, and those who did not have any treaty or had an indeterminate period of expiration were given four months' period of grace, and terms were kept with those with whom the treaty was due to expire. All the latter people embraced Islam even before the term expired, and the non-Muslims of the state paid Jizyah"<br /><br />Qutb's third point is that the only true civilization is to be found in Islam. Here, he gets rather vague, but seeks to ram home his point by way of repetition: "Islam establishes the values and morals which are 'human' - those which develop characteristics in a human being which distinguish him from the animals. In whatever society Islam is dominant, whether it is an agricultural or industrial society, nomadic and pastoral or urban and settled, poor or rich, it implants these human values and morlas, nurtures them and strengthens them; it develops human characteristics progressively and guards against degeneration toward animalism". In fact, Qutb repeats his points to the degree that a text which could easily be boiled down to 10-20 pages extends to a lengthy 160. <br /><br />For good measure, Qutb throws in a little bit of anti-semitism at the end, claiming the statement that "culture is the human heritage" to be "one of the tricks played by world Jewry, whose purpose is to eliminate all limitations, especially the limitations imposed by faith and religion, so that the Jews may penetrate into body politic of the whole world and then may be free to perpetuate their evil designs. At the top of the list of these activities is usury, the aim of which is that all the wealth of mankind end up in the hands of Jewish financial institutions which run on interest". Sensible words from a man who, according to his own description, "has spent forty years of his life in reading books and in research in almost all aspects of human knowledge". <br /><br />In short, I am not very impressed by this book. As most religious rabble I've read, it consits merely of dogmatic statements about the past and claims about a glorious future which are more or less devoid of substance. The concept of the Islamic state remains as elusive as ever to me after reading this book, and as long as its foremost thinker is unable to provide any answers as to how the Islamic state would deal with global warming or universal healthcare, I am inclined to stick to the current political system in which I'm apparently subdued and blinded, yet housed, clothed, healthy and fed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11293889880624523230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText15381661146963672039" style="display:none">Qutb wrote this book as a series of checkpoints and milestones that need to be reached on the way to creating a true Muslim society. He discussed the political, personal and social changes that need to take place. And then I procrastinated starting it until days before it was due back.<br /><br />Why I finished it: I haven't read many religious books outside of my own faith, and it was a struggle for me... and honestly I couldn't tell if it was the LDS vs. Muslim, 1950s vs. 2019, or the American vs. Egyptian that I couldn't translate. It was frustrating to read, knowing that I understood all the words but also knowing that I was missing meaning and context.<br /><br />Points that Qutb made:<br />1. Islam is about belief. It is not about race or nationality. Muslim brotherhood supersedes genetics and nationality.<br />2. No one comes between a believer and God but a true Muslim must learn science and religion from another Muslim. <br />3. It takes time to form a Muslim community.</span>

<div id="review_168121220" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168121220" />
    <a title="Idealist- Realist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5432179-idealist--realist"><img alt="Idealist- Realist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580117946p2/5432179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168121220?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Idealist- Realist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Idealist- Realist" href="/user/show/5432179-idealist--realist">Idealist- Realist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788172312442" href="/book/show/298040.Milestones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5432179-idealist--realist?shelf=motivational-self-help-inspirationa">motivational-self-help-inspirationa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5432179-idealist--realist?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5432179-idealist--realist?shelf=politics">politics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168121220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11516383759589447426">The logic I rate this book with only four is not because I don’t agree with Qutb ideas but maybe because I have some confusions regarding the nature of the Islamic mission due to my limited and scarce knowledge about Islam. <br/><br/>The book is deemed to be one of the seminal and influential books in history. It is important to note that Qutb is a vanguard of the movement Muslim Brotherhood after the founder Imam As-shaheed Hassan al-Banna. From his book milestones, we can gauge what is the stance of the</span>
  <span id="freeText11516383759589447426" style="display:none">The logic I rate this book with only four is not because I don’t agree with Qutb ideas but maybe because I have some confusions regarding the nature of the Islamic mission due to my limited and scarce knowledge about Islam. <br /><br />The book is deemed to be one of the seminal and influential books in history. It is important to note that Qutb is a vanguard of the movement Muslim Brotherhood after the founder Imam As-shaheed Hassan al-Banna. From his book milestones, we can gauge what is the stance of the Islamic revivalist movement although there are some changes and shifts in their strategies due their political experience and societal conditions.<br /><br />If you don’t widen your perspective while reading this book, you’ll undoubtedly raise some questions about his rigid and extreme ideas. If I may mention some of them; He said that there is no room for Islam to compromise with Jahiliyyah. NEVER!, his criticism on Muslims whom he called defeatist and apologetic faced with the attacks of the orientalists. This defeated mentality tries to justify Islam on defensive side whereas according to Qutb Islam is not defensive but it is to destroy the institution and system of Jahili system. This is the part which I am confused<br /><br />Clearly enough, the Islamic mission is to liberate man from servitude to other humans. When mankind is totally submissive to the sovereignty of Allah alone, only then we can taste the true essence of freedom. This is the clear of message of Islam. To carry out the message or goals, Islamic movement is a necessity. This Islamic group will translate beliefs into living reality. And it should not confine its approach only through preaching but also through movement. To me, what he meant here by movement is a physical jihad. Force according to him is part of the means of the Islamic group. He made mention the historical example during Prophet’s (S.A.W.) time. In the time of Prophet, there were series of wars initiated by Muslims. When there was a time to refrain from war like that of Medina time, that was only a strategy to prepare themselves for future wars. The Muslim community cannot be formed or continue to exist without resources or sufficient power. From this, there should be enough power to confront jahiliyyah. It entails tact, wisdom and logic to defy it. <br /><br />On education and knowledge, Qutb categorized the sciences that Muslims are allowed to learn himself alone, with the help of pious scholars or abandoning totally the discipline. First, A Muslim cannot go to any source other than God for guidance in matters of faith, in the concept of life, acts of worship, morals and human affairs, values and standards, principles of economics and political affairs and interpretation of historical processes. Secondly, a Muslim can go to a Muslim or to a non-Muslim to learn ABSTRACT SCIENCES such as chemistry, physics, biology, astronomy, medicine, industry, agriculture, administration (limited to its technical aspects), technology, military arts and similar sciences and arts as all these sciences and arts are a sufficient obligation (Fard al-Kifayah) on Muslims. With regards to interpretation of human endeavor whether this endeavor be individual or collective, a Muslim should not rely upon it for this relates to theories of the nature of man and of historical processes. Similarly, the explanation of the origin of universe, the origin of the life of man are part of metaphysics which Muslims are not allowed to study or believed in since there is only source (Divinely-revealed knowledge). However, Qutb did not stop there from disallowing Muslim to study any thing related to Human interpretations and speculation not guided God. He postulated that a Muslim can study all opinions and thoughts of Jahili writers, not from the point of view of constructing his own beliefs and concepts, but for the purpose of knowing the deviations adopted by Jahiliyyah.<br /><br />To sum up, his book milestones is intended to the vanguards of Islamic movement to carry out the Islamic mission on earth. He did not describe what those milestones are but it is for the workers of Islam to fathom and it requires deep thoughts. It is a natural tendency for the callers of Islam while marching on the road that there are torments, sufferings, tortures will be inflicted upon them by tyrants and criminals. To ponder on this reality, here is a comment of Qur’an on the story of Pit where God Most High says:<br />“And they were angered with the Believers, only because they believed in God, the All-Powerful, the All-Praiseworthy.”<br /><br />As I made mention earlier, reading Qutb works requires a wider perspective, vast understanding about Islam and knowledge about his experience. One thing to understand his works is to understand also his experiences. Most of his works are done while he was in prison and he was tortured because of his Islamic mission. Under the clutches of the tyrants, you would expect him to write with rigidity and extremism. His use of language is very striking. However, he did not miss to point out the intricate point along the road of the vanguard of Islam requires deep thought by all callers of Islam . Also, there should be sufficient power for Islamic movement to continue to exist to translate its belief to living reality. From these points, we can gauge that we need to consider the present condition of muslims and the nature of international system. Even though he said never to compromise with Jahili system but he was somehow flexible as we can extract from those points I just mentioned. One thing worthy of mentioning is his notion of the offensive nature of Islam that it imperative to wage Physical force to destroy institution and the system in particular. This is the point I am so confused about. However, if you look at the present condition of the international system, it is oppressive in nature that it only serves their interest that it talks human rights when it is in their favor. This in totality puts Islam on its defensive side.<br /><br />IT REQUIRES A DEEP THOUGHT</span>
  <a data-text-id="11516383759589447426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2629931771" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2629931771" />
    <a title="Grace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58735562-grace"><img alt="Grace" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2629931771?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
